Islam and Iman: Three Opinions and a Path Forward
Ghazali introduces the dispute over Islam and Iman and summarizes three opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three scholarly opinions on the relationship between Islam and Iman and Ghazali's methodological approach to resolving the dispute.

Core Idea
The relationship between Islam and Iman is not merely semantic but has deep theological implications.

Ghazali begins by presenting a classical theological debate: are Islam and Iman identical or distinct? He summarizes three opinions and criticizes previous treatments as confused. He then outlines a systematic three-part investigation starting with the linguistic meanings of the terms. This lesson sets the stage for a rigorous analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: the issue, the opinions, the criticism, the announcement of investigations.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three opinions
List the three opinions Ghazali reports: (1) they are one thing, (2) two separate things, (3) two things but connected. Write them in your own words.
3
Understand Ghazali's criticism
Ghazali dismisses Abu Talib al-Makki's treatment as confused and lengthy. He wants to proceed directly to the truth. Ask: Why might a clear method be important here?
This shows Ghazali's commitment to clarity.
4
Preview the three investigations
Ghazali announces three investigations (mabāhith). The first is the linguistic import of the two words. Note that only the first is introduced in this passage; the others will follow.
This sets up the coming lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة :
إختلفوا في أن الإسلام هو الإيمان أو غيره ، وإن كان غيره فهل هو منفصل عنه يوجد دونه أو مرتبط به يلازمه :
فقيل : إنهما شيء واحد
وقيل : إنهما شيآن لا يتواصلان
وقيل : إنهما شيآن ولكن يرتبط أحدهما بالآخر
وقد أورد أبو طالب المكي في هذا كلاماً شديد الإضطراب كثير التطويل
فلنهجم الآن على التصريح بالحق من غير تعريج على نقل ما لا تحصيل له ، فنقول :
في هذا ثلاثة مباحث :
بحث عن موجب اللفظين في اللغة
Issue: They differed concerning whether Islam is Iman or something else, and if it is something else, then is it separate from it, existing without it, or connected to it, inseparable from it? So it was said: they are one thing. And it was said: they are two things that do not connect. And it was said: they are two things but one of them is connected to the other. And Abu Talib al-Makki has reported on this extremely confused and lengthy speech. So let us now proceed to state the truth clearly without lingering on narrating what is useless. So we say: In this there are three investigations: an investigation of the import of the two words in language.
Source pages p. 234

Commentary
This passage introduces a theological issue: whether Islam and Iman (faith) are identical or different. Ghazali first states the question: are they the same or distinct? If distinct, are they separable or inseparable? He then summarizes three opinions: (1) they are one and the same; (2) they are two separate things that do not connect; (3) they are two things but one is connected to the other. He then criticizes the treatment of this issue by Abu Talib al-Makki as confusing and lengthy. To avoid such confusion, Ghazali announces his intention to clarify the truth directly. He then outlines three investigations (mabāhith) to be conducted, beginning with the first: an examination of the linguistic import (mūjib) of the two terms. This sets the stage for a systematic linguistic and theological analysis.

Key Terms
الإسلام
الإيمان
موجب اللفظين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the issue is merely semantic and that Islam and Iman are always identical.
Correction: Ghazali shows that scholars held diverse views with real theological implications; the relationship is not automatically one of identity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT one of the opinions mentioned regarding Islam and Iman?
A) They are one thing.
B) They are two things that do not connect.
C) They are two things but one is connected to the other.
D) They are one thing in name but two in reality.

Key Takeaway
The relationship between Islam and Iman is a debated topic with three classical opinions, and Ghazali stresses the need for a clear, systematic investigation beginning with language.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own life, do you see a gap between your outward actions of Islam (prayer, fasting, etc.) and your inner state of Iman (conviction, trust, love)? Which of the three opinions—identical, separate, or connected—best describes your personal experience? And what would it take for you to move toward the connected view that Ghazali seems to favor?
?
Ghazali criticizes 'confused and lengthy speech' that leads nowhere. Have you ever found yourself arguing about definitions or theological points while neglecting the reality of your own relationship with Allah? How can you shift from debate to sincere self-examination starting today?
?
If you were to honestly assess your Iman right now, would you say it is truly connected to your Islam? What specific habit or attitude in your life is evidence that the two are either aligned or disconnected? After this lesson, what one change will you make to strengthen that connection?

Three Investigations: A Framework for Islam and Iman
Ghazali introduces three investigations and their classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct investigations Ghazali proposes to resolve the debate about the relationship between Islam and Iman.

Core Idea
Ghazali cuts through confusion by identifying three separate angles: linguistic, exegetical, and juristic.

Ghazali opens by noting the widespread confusion among scholars about whether Islam and Iman are the same or different. Instead of reviewing endless and unproductive opinions, he announces a methodical approach: three investigations that will clarify the truth. This lesson presents that essential framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening issue
Read the passage carefully and note the three opinions Ghazali mentions about Islam and Iman.
Notice the criticism of Abu Talib al-Makki's lengthy treatment.
2
Identify the three investigations
Find the sentence where Ghazali lists the three investigations and their descriptions.
Each investigation has a different scope: language, Quranic usage, and juristic rulings.
3
Understand the order
Ask yourself why the linguistic investigation comes first and the juristic last.
The linguistic meaning grounds the exegetical and juristic applications.
4
Reflect on the benefit
Consider how separating these angles helps avoid confusion in theological discussions.
This is a key methodological insight from Ghazali.

(۲) لأنه يبحث فيه عن إطلاقات القرآن الكريم .
(۳) لأنه يبحث فيه عما يترتب على المتصف بهما ثواباً وعقاباً
(٤) بفتح الجيم من أوجب عليه كذا ، فهو موجب ، والمعنى ما يوجبه اللغة إيجاباً ، والموجب بالكسر هو الذي يجب صدور الفعل عنه ، بأن كان علة تامة له من غير قصد وإرادة وهذا هو الموجب بالذات ومثلوه بوجوب صدور الإحراق من النار ، ويراد بهذا المفهوم وهو ما دل عليه اللفظ لا في محل النطق . (٥) وهو أن تنسب بإختيارك الصدق إلى المخبر أو المخبر عنه ، والصدق مطابقة القول الضمير والمعبر عنه معنى ، ثم إستعماله في التصديق إما مجاز لقوي أو حقيقة لغوية .
(٦) الآية ١٧ من سورة يوسف وتمامها : ﴿ وَلَوْ كُنَّا صَادِقِينَ )
(۷) التسليم : هو ترك الإعتراض فيما لا يلائم ، والإستسلام : هو الإنقياد الظاهر فقط والدخول في السلم .
(۸) الإباء : الكراهية والإمتناع ؛ والعناد : هو المبالغة في الأعراض ومخالفة الحق ،
(۹) الذي يعبر عن ذلك المعني القائم بالقلب
Issue: They differed concerning whether Islam is Iman or something else. If it is something else, is it separate from it, existing without it, or connected to it, inseparable from it? So it was said: They are one thing (1). And it was said: They are two things that are not connected (2). And it was said: They are two things but one is connected to the other. Abu Talib al-Makki (3) presented on this matter speech of great confusion and much prolixity (4). So let us now rush (5) to declare the truth without turning to transmitting that which yields no benefit. So we say: In this there are three investigations: An investigation into the signification (muujib) of the two words in language (6). (1) One thing in meaning and ruling; one is used for the other. (2) They are not connected; rather they are independent in themselves. (3) He is Muhammad ibn Ali ibn Atiyya al-Harithi al-Basri al-Makki, in his book Qut al-Qulub. (4) Much prolixity in citing expressions, and whatever is like that is of little benefit. (5) 'Falanajim': from al-hajm, which is entering once quickly. (6) With the opening of the letter jim from al-mujib. And an investigation into its ruling in this world and the Hereafter. And the first investigation is linguistic (1), the second is exegetical (2), and the third is juristic (3). The first investigation: concerning the signification (muujib) of language (4). The truth in it is that Iman is tasdiq (5). Allah the Exalted said (6): 'And you are not a believer (mu'min) for us' i.e. a tasdiqer. And Islam is taslim and istislam with dhudhan (7), compliance, and leaving obstinacy and refusal (8) out of stubbornness. And tasdiq has a specific place: the heart, and the tongue is its interpreter (9). (1) Because in it is investigated the essence of the two words. (2) Because in it is investigated the usages of the Noble Quran. (3) Because in it is investigated what is entailed for the one described by them of reward and punishment. (4) With the opening of the letter jim from 'awjaba' upon him something, so it is 'muujib' (obligator). The meaning is what the language obliges as an obligation. And 'al-muujib' with a kasrah is that from which the act must issue, as it is a complete cause without intention and will—this is the necessitator by essence, and they gave an example of the necessity of burning from fire. What is intended by this concept is what the word indicates, not in the place of utterance. (5) It is that you ascribe, by your choice, truthfulness to the informant or the informed about. And truthfulness (sidq) is the correspondence of the utterance to the conscience and what is expressed in meaning. Then its use for tasdiq is either a strong metaphor or a linguistic reality. (6) Verse 17 of Surah Yusuf, and its completion: 'And we were truthful'. (7) Taslim is refraining from objection to what is not agreeable. Istislam is only apparent compliance and entering into peace. (8) Iba' is dislike and refusal; 'inad is exaggeration in aversion and opposing the truth. (9) That which expresses that meaning subsisting in the heart.
Source pages p. 235

Commentary
This passage identifies a classification and definition. It begins by stating the issue: whether Islam and Iman are the same or different, and if different, whether they are separate or connected. Ghazali mentions the opinions and criticizes Abu Talib al-Makki's lengthy and confusing treatment. He then declares he will go straight to the truth, presenting three investigations: (1) the linguistic signification of the two words, (2) their exegetical usage in the Quran, and (3) their jurisprudential effect in this world and the next. He begins the first investigation. For Iman, he says it is tasdiq (assent of the heart), citing Quran 12:17. For Islam, he says it is taslim (submission) and istislam (surrender) with compliance and acceptance, leaving obstinacy and refusal. He then notes that tasdiq resides in the heart, with the tongue as its interpreter. The footnotes provide additional clarification: e.g., the distinction between muujib (obligator) and mujib (necessitator), the definition of sidq as correspondence, and the meanings of key terms.

Key Terms

إسلام
موجب
تفسيري
فقهي

D) Lexical, syntactic, and rhetorical

Key Takeaway
Ghazali resolves the confusion about Islam and Iman by separating the discussion into three clear, ordered investigations: linguistic, exegetical, and juristic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a confusing religious issue, do you instinctively jump to a verdict, or do you patiently separate the linguistic, Quranic, and practical angles? Which of these three investigations have you neglected most, and what will you do to correct that this week?
?
Ghazali criticizes long-winded, confused writing. Is your own understanding of faith muddled by mixing different types of questions? Today, identify one area where you have conflated 'what a word means' with 'what the Quran says' and commit to untangling them.
?
You will stand before Allah with your belief. After seeing Ghazali's clear method, can you honestly say you have taken the time to distinguish the linguistic meaning of Iman from its Quranic usage and its legal consequences? If not, what is the first concrete step you will take in the next 24 hours?

Linguistic Definitions: Iman as Tasdiq, Islam as Taslim
Ghazali gives linguistic definitions of Iman and Islam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic meanings of Iman and Islam according to Ghazali, including the role of the heart and tongue.

Core Idea
Iman is heartfelt assent; Islam is outward submission.

Having established the three-part framework, Ghazali now turns to the first investigation: the linguistic signification of the words Iman and Islam. He provides clear definitions grounded in Quranic usage and Arabic lexicography. This lesson unpacks those definitions and explains where true faith resides.

Guided Path
1
Read the linguistic definitions
Read the passage and note how Ghazali defines Iman and Islam separately, with Quranic evidence.
Pay attention to the Quranic verse he cites for Iman.
2
Understand Iman as tasdiq
Analyze the concept of tasdiq (assenting/declaring true) and its place in the heart.
The footnotes explain tasdiq as ascribing truthfulness by choice.
3
Understand Islam as taslim and istislam
Explore the meanings of taslim (submission) and istislam (surrender) with compliance and leaving obstinacy.
The footnote distinguishes between taslim and istislam.
4
Recognize the heart-tongue relationship
Note Ghazali's statement that tasdiq has a specific place (the heart) and the tongue is its interpreter.
This implies that outward expression is secondary to inner conviction.
5
Reflect on the distinction
Consider how these linguistic definitions lay the groundwork for the exegetical and juristic investigations.
The linguistic meaning is the foundation for later discussions.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Iman is merely verbal confession without inner conviction.
Correction: Ghazali defines Iman as tasdiq (heartfelt assent), and states that the tongue is only its interpreter, so the essence of Iman is internal.

?

?

?
Ghazali says the tongue is just the interpreter of the heart. If your tongue speaks shahada but your heart is full of doubt or disobedience, you are living a contradiction. Will you sit with your heart today and ask it: 'Why do you not fully assent to what you claim?' Then commit to one concrete action that moves your heart closer to genuine tasdiq.

Linguistic Definitions of Iman and Islam and Their Relationship
Ghazali defines Iman and Islam linguistically and shows Islam is more general.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic distinction between Iman (faith) and Islam as defined by Imam al-Ghazali, and how Islam is more general in scope.

Core Idea
Islam is the broader category; Iman is its most essential part.

Imam al-Ghazali begins his theological inquiry by clarifying the very words we use. Before we can discuss rulings on faith and Islam, we must understand what these terms mean in the language. This passage establishes the linguistic foundations: Iman is affirmation of the heart, while Islam is a broader submission that includes heart, tongue, and limbs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: Ghazali first categorizes the investigation, then defines terms, then shows their relationship.
Pay attention to the footnote from Imam al-Subki for an alternative view.
2
Identify the definitions of Iman and Islam
Find how Ghazali defines Iman (tasdiq) and Islam (taslim/istislam). Note the Quranic evidence for Iman and the explanation of the loci of these concepts.
Tasdiq is specific to the heart; taslim is general to heart, tongue, and limbs.
3
Understand the relationship
Extract the conclusion: Islam is more general (aam) and Iman is more specific (akhass). Thus every Iman is Islam, but not every Islam is Iman. Reflect on the logical implications.
This relationship is a key principle in Kalam.
4
Consider the footnote from Imam al-Subki
Imam al-Subki offers a nuanced view of mutual entailment. Compare his position with Ghazali's straightforward linguistic imperative.
This shows how scholars build on earlier definitions.

وأما التسليم فإنه عام في القلب واللسان والجوارح ، فإن كل تصديق بالقلب فهو تسليم وترك الأباء والجحود ، وكذلك الإعتراف باللسان ، وكذلك الطاعة والإنقياد بالجوارح .
فموجب اللغة أن الإسلام أعم ، والإيمان أخص ، فكان الإيمان عبارة عن أشرف أجزاء الإسلام ، فإذن كل تصديق تسليم ، وليس كل تسليم تصديقاً (۱)
(1) يقول الإمام السبكي رضي الله عنه ورحمه :
إشتهر المغايرة بالعموم والخصوص المطلق ، فكل إيمان إسلام ، ولا ينعكس ، ثم إختار أن الظاهر تساويهما أو تلازمهما بمعنى أن الإسلام موضوع لإنقياد الظاهر مشروطاً فيه الإيمان ، والإيمان موضوع للتصديق الباطن مشروطاً فيه القول عند الإمكان ، فثبت تلازمهما وتقايرهما ؛
ولا يقال : كل إيمان إسلام ولا كل إسلام إيمان ، ولا تنافي أن يكون المتباينان متلازمين ، لأن معنى التباين أن لا يصدقا على ذات واحدة ، وإن تلازماً في الوجود ، هذا في الإسلام المعتدية وقول من قال : كل إيمان إسلام ولا عكس أطلق الإسلام على ما يعتد به وعلى ما لا يعتد به ، ثم فيه ذلك تجوز وتحرير العبارة أن يقال : كل إيمان يلزمه الإسلام ولا ينعكس .
مع
•
وأما قول من قال : كل مؤمن مسلم ولا ينعكس ، فإن جعلت الإيمان لا يحصل مسماه إلا بشرط اللفظ فيصح وإن جعلته يحصل مسماه لكن لا يعتد به شرعاً إلا بالتلفظ لا يصح
And it investigates its ruling in this world and the Hereafter. The first investigation is linguistic (1), the second is exegetical (2), and the third is juristic-legal. The first investigation concerns the linguistic imperative (4). The truth in it is that Iman is an expression for tasdiq (affirmation) (5). Allah Most High said (6): "And you are not a believer in us" — i.e., not a confirmer. (3) And Islam is an expression for submission and surrender by compliance (7), obedience, and abandoning stubbornness and refusal (8) out of obstinacy. Tasdiq has a specific locus, which is the heart, and the tongue is its interpreter (9). (1) Because it investigates the essence of the two words. (2) Because it investigates the usages of the Noble Quran. (3) Because it investigates what follows for one described by them in terms of reward and punishment. (4) With a fatha on the jim: from 'awjaba upon him such-and-such, so it is mujib; the meaning is what language necessitates necessarily. Al-mūjib with kasra is that from which an action must proceed, being a complete cause without intention and will, and this is the necessitator by essence, and they gave the example of the necessity of fire producing heat. What is meant by this concept is what the word indicates, not in the place of utterance. (5) It is that you, by your choice, attribute truthfulness to the informant or the informed-about; truthfulness is the correspondence of the statement to the conscience and the meaning expressed; then its use in tasdiq is either a strong metaphor or a literal linguistic fact. (6) Verse 17 of Surah Yusuf, its completion: "And we were truthful." (7) Taslim is refraining from objection regarding what is incompatible; istislam is outward obedience only and entering into peace. (8) Iba' is dislike and refusal; 'inad is exaggeration in turning away and opposing the truth. (9) That which expresses that meaning residing in the heart. As for taslim, it is general in the heart, tongue, and limbs. For every tasdiq of the heart is a taslim and a leaving of refusal and denial, likewise confession by the tongue, likewise obedience and submission by the limbs. So the linguistic imperative is that Islam is more general and Iman is more specific; thus Iman is an expression for the noblest parts of Islam. Therefore every tasdiq is a taslim, but not every taslim is a tasdiq (1). (1) Imam al-Subki (may Allah be pleased with him and have mercy on him) says: The distinction by absolute generality and specificity became well-known: every Iman is Islam, but not the reverse. Then he chose that the apparent view is that they are equal or mutually entailing, in the sense that Islam is placed for outward submission conditioned by Iman, and Iman is placed for inward tasdiq conditioned by utterance when possible. So their mutual entailment and distinctness are established. And it is not said: every Iman is Islam nor every Islam is Iman. There is no contradiction that two distinct things are mutually entailing, because the meaning of distinctness is that they are not both true of one entity, even if they entail each other in existence. This applies to valid Islam. The statement of those who say: every Iman is Islam but not the reverse applies Islam to what is valid and what is not valid; then that involves metaphor. The precise expression is to say: every Iman entails Islam, but not the reverse. As for the statement of those who say: every believer is a Muslim but not the reverse: if you consider that Iman does not attain its meaning except with the condition of utterance, it holds; but if you consider that its meaning is attained but it is not valid legally except with utterance, it does not hold.
Source pages p. 236

Commentary
This passage is a careful linguistic investigation of the terms 'Iman' and 'Islam'. Ghazali begins by stating that the overall discussion examines the ruling of these terms in this world and the Hereafter, and then he divides the investigation into three parts: linguistic, exegetical (based on Quranic usage), and juristic-legal. He then focuses on the first: the linguistic imperative. The key claim is that Iman is essentially tasdiq (affirmation of truth), supported by the Quranic verse 'you are not a believer in us' meaning 'you are not a confirmer'. Islam is defined as taslim (submission) and istislam (surrender) through compliance, obedience, and abandoning stubbornness. Tasdiq has a specific locus: the heart; the tongue is its interpreter. Ghazali then explains that taslim (submission) applies generally to the heart, tongue, and limbs: every inner tasdiq is a form of taslim, and likewise verbal confession and physical obedience are forms of taslim. Therefore, the linguistic principle is that Islam is more general (aam) and Iman is more specific (akhass); Iman is the noblest part of Islam. Consequently, every tasdiq is a taslim, but not every taslim is a tasdiq. The footnote from Imam al-Subki elaborates on the relationship: he acknowledges the common view of absolute generality (every Iman is Islam, not the reverse), but suggests that the apparent view is mutual entailment—Islam is outward submission conditioned by Iman, and Iman is inward tasdiq conditioned by utterance when possible. Subki clarifies that one can say 'every Iman entails Islam' without the reverse being necessarily true, and he addresses the condition of utterance for the validity of Iman in legal terms.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Iman and Islam are synonyms in Sharia, both meaning 'faith' interchangeably.
Correction: Ghazali shows linguistically that Islam is more general, encompassing submission in heart, tongue, and limbs, while Iman is specifically the affirmation of the heart.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's linguistic analysis, what is the relationship between Iman and Islam?
A) Iman is more general than Islam.
B) Islam is more general than Iman.
C) They are perfect synonyms.
D) They are completely different with no overlap.

Key Takeaway
Linguistically, Islam is the broader term encompassing submission of heart, tongue, and limbs, while Iman is the specific inner affirmation of the heart; thus every Iman is Islam but not every Islam is Iman.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call yourself a Muslim, do you find that your submission is only external—going through the motions—while inside you resist or doubt? What is one area where your heart refuses to truly submit to Allah's command?
?
If Iman is primarily inner affirmation of the heart, ask yourself: do you truly affirm in your heart that everything Allah and His Messenger have said is true, even the parts that contradict your desires? If not, where does your loyalty lie?
?
Every tasdiq is a taslim, but not every taslim is a tasdiq. Is your Islam just a taslim of the tongue and limbs, or does it spring from a heart that is fully surrendered? What would it take for your heart to match your outward actions?

Evidence for Synonymy of Islam and Iman
Al-Ghazali cites Quran and Hadith to prove that Islam and Iman are synonymous.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic and Hadith evidence that Al-Ghazali uses to establish that 'Islam' and 'Iman' are synonymous terms.

Core Idea
The Quran and Hadith use 'Islam' and 'Iman' interchangeably, showing they are one reality.

In this lesson, Al-Ghazali presents three proofs from revelation to demonstrate that 'Islam' and 'Iman' are not separate things but refer to the same underlying reality. He cites two Quranic verses and a hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) defined faith as the five pillars of Islam. This passage builds a strong case for synonymy, which has important implications for theological discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Note the three distinct proofs Al-Ghazali presents.
Pay attention to how each proof uses both terms in parallel.
2
Identify the three proofs
Find the two Quranic verses and the Hadith. Notice how each one pairs 'Islam' and 'Iman' in a way that suggests they refer to the same thing.
The first proof uses exception (except the house of Muslims), the second addresses believers as Muslims, and the third defines faith as the pillars of Islam.
3
Understand the conclusion
Al-Ghazali concludes that 'Islam is faith' (al-islam huwa al-iman). Reflect on why this synonymy matters for the study of Islamic creed.
If they are synonyms, there is no essential difference between being a Muslim and a believer.
4
Consider the implications
Think about what happens if someone distinguishes between Islam and Iman. This passage guards against splitting faith into separate categories.
This lesson connects to broader discussions in kalam about the relationship between outward submission and inward conviction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الترادف ففي قوله تعالى : فَأَخْرَجْنَا مَن كَانَ فِيهَا مِنَ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ فَمَا وَجَدْنَا فِيهَا غَيْرَ بَيْتٍ مِّنَ الْمُسْلِمِينَ ولم يكن بالإتفاق إلا بيت واحد. وقال تعالى : يا قوم إن كُنتُم آمَنَتُم بِاللهِ فَعَلَيْهِ تَوَكَّلُوا إِن كُنتُم مُسْلِمِينَ. وقال : « بني الإسلام على خمس. وسئل رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم مرة عن الإيمان، فأجاب بهذه الخمس. فيكون الإسلام هو الإيمان.
As for synonymy, it is in His statement the Exalted: 'So We brought out whoever was in it of the believers, and We found not in it other than a house of the Muslims' — and it was not by agreement except one house. And He said the Exalted: 'O my people, if you have believed in Allah, then rely upon Him, if you are Muslims.' And He said: 'Islam is built upon five.' And the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, was asked once about faith (al-iman), and he answered with these five. So Islam is faith (al-iman).
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage presents the evidence for the synonymy (taraduf) of the terms 'Islam' and 'Iman'. The author, Al-Ghazali, begins by citing three proofs. First, the Quranic verse from Surat al-Dhariyat where Allah mentions 'believers' (mu'minin) and then immediately after refers to them as 'Muslims' (muslimin) in the same context, implying interchangeability. Second, the verse from Surat Yunus where Allah addresses believers as 'Muslims', again using the terms in parallel. Third, a hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) was asked about faith (iman) and he responded with the five pillars of Islam, indicating that the content of faith is the same as the content of Islam. The author concludes that Islam and Iman are identical (fayakunu al-islam huwa al-iman).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Islam and Iman are completely separate concepts, one being outward and the other inward.
Correction: The Quran and Hadith use both terms synonymously, as shown in these proofs; they refer to the same essential reality of submission to Allah.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali uses three textual proofs to demonstrate that 'Islam' and 'Iman' are synonymous in Islamic revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

When was the last time you performed a pillar (like prayer or charity) with the conscious intention of strengthening your iman? Commit to that intention for your next prayer.
?
Are there areas of your life where you separate 'being a Muslim' from 'having true faith'—perhaps treating some duties as merely cultural or habitual? How can you bring them together starting today, following the example of the Quran and Hadith?

Evidence that Islam and Iman are Distinct
Al-Ghazali cites the verse of the Bedouins and the Hadith of Jibril to differentiate Islam from Iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran and hadith establish that Islam (outward submission) is distinct from Iman (inner faith).

Core Idea
Outward Islam can exist without inner Iman — they are not the same.

Al-Ghazali now turns to prove the difference between Islam and Iman using two authoritative sources: the Quran and the Hadith of Jibril. This passage anchors the distinction in revelation, showing that the terms refer to different realities: outward surrender and inner conviction. Understanding this difference prevents confusion in judging people’s faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the full translation to capture the flow of Al-Ghazali's argument.
Notice how each proof is introduced concisely.
2
Identify the two proofs
The passage presents two distinct proofs: the Quranic verse about the Bedouins, and the Hadith of Jibril. Locate each and note how the author uses them.
Each proof defines the terms differently.
3
Understand the definitions given
In the verse, Iman is inner assent (tasdiq) and Islam is outward surrender (istislam). In the hadith, Iman is belief in six articles and Islam is the five pillars. See the pattern: Iman is inward, Islam is outward.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Al-Ghazali concludes that these differences in definition prove that Islam and Iman are distinct in their rulings (hukm). They are not interchangeable terms.
This distinction matters for theology and jurisprudence.
5
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Can someone be a Muslim (outwardly) without being a true believer (inwardly)? The verse shows yes. This guards against judging hearts.
This is the practical takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الإختلاف فقوله تعالى : قَالَتِ الْأَعْرَابُ آمَنَّا قُل لَّمْ تُؤْمِنُوا وَلَكِن قُولُوا أَسْلَمْنَا ومعناه استسلمنا في الظاهر، فأراد الإيمان ها هنا التصديق فقط وبالإسلام الإستسلام ظاهراً باللسان والجوارح. وفي حديث جبرائيل عليه السلام لما سأله عن الإيمان ، فقال : أن نؤمن بالله وملائكته وكتبه ورسله واليوم الآخر ، وبالبعث بعد الموت وبالحساب وبالقدر خيره وشره فقال : فما الإسلام ؟ فأجاب بذكر الخصال الخمس فعبر بالإسلام عن تسليم الظاهر بالقول والعمل فدل على إختلافهما في الحكم.
As for the difference, it is in His statement the Exalted: 'The Bedouins said, "We have believed." Say, "You have not believed, but say, "We have submitted." ' Its meaning is: we have surrendered outwardly. So He intended by 'iman' here only the inner assent (tasdiq), and by 'islam' outward surrender (istislam) by the tongue and limbs. And in the hadith of Jibril, peace be upon him, when he asked about faith (al-iman), he (the Prophet) said: 'That we believe in Allah, His angels, His books, His messengers, the Last Day, and the resurrection after death, and the reckoning, and the divine decree, its good and its evil.' Then he said: 'What is Islam?' So he answered by mentioning the five characteristics, and he expressed by 'islam' the outward submission (taslim) by speech and action. So this indicates their difference in ruling.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage presents the evidence for the difference (ikhtilaf) between Islam and Iman. Al-Ghazali cites two proofs. First, the Quranic verse from Surat al-Hujurat where Allah instructs the believers to say 'We have submitted' (aslamna) instead of 'We have believed' (amanna), because their belief was not genuine. This shows that Islam can exist without true Iman; Islam is outward surrender (istislam) while Iman is inner assent (tasdiq). Second, the famous hadith of Jibril where the Prophet (peace be upon him) defined Iman as belief in the six articles of faith, and defined Islam as the five pillars. Since the definitions are different, they are distinct in reality. The author concludes that these two terms differ in their rulings (hukm), meaning they have different meanings and different requirements.

Key Terms
اختلاف (ikhtilaf)
الإيمان (al-iman)
الإسلام (al-islam)
التصديق (al-tasdiq)
الاستسلام (al-istislam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Islam and Iman are the same thing, so outward profession equals true faith.
Correction: The Quran and hadith show that a person can be Muslim in outward submission without having genuine inner faith; they are distinct categories.

Checkpoint
In the hadith of Jibril, how does the Prophet distinguish between Iman and Islam?
A) Iman is outward submission, Islam is inner assent
B) Iman is belief in the six pillars, Islam is the five pillars
C) Iman is only for the Bedouins, Islam is for others
D) Iman and Islam are the same

Key Takeaway
The Quran and the Hadith of Jibril definitively separate the concepts of Islam (outward submission) and Iman (inner faith), showing they have different meanings and rulings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have said 'I submit' many times in your prayers and daily life. But have you ever actually come before Allah with the silence of a heart that truly believes—without the noise of performance? When was the last time you felt genuine tasdiq, not just going through the motions? Will you ask Allah tonight to replace your istislam with iman?
?
The Bedouins were told their claim of iman was false, yet they were still called Muslims. Is there an area of your deen where you are content with being a 'Bedouin'—doing the actions but refusing to let your heart be transformed? What is holding you back from seeking the deeper tasdiq?
?
The Prophet defined iman as believing in six things, and islam as doing five things. Which of those six beliefs do you struggle to truly internalize? When you say 'I believe in the Last Day,' does it change how you live today? If not, what are you really trusting in?

Three Linguistic Usages of Islam and Iman
Ghazali explains the three permissible linguistic usages of Islam and Iman with proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three permissible linguistic usages of the terms 'Islam' and 'Iman' in Arabic and how they are supported by hadith and Quran.

Core Idea
The words 'Islam' and 'Iman' can be used in three distinct ways — all valid in Arabic.

Why can a person be called a Muslim without being a true believer? Imam al-Ghazali examines the linguistic flexibility of two key terms. In this passage, he shows that the relationship between Islam and Iman can be understood as difference, overlap, or synonymy — and all are permissible within Arabic figurative language.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith examples
Start by reading the two hadiths cited by Ghazali. Notice how the Prophet distinguishes between 'Muslim' and 'believer' in the first, and ranks Iman as the best part of Islam in the second.
These establish difference and overlap.
2
Identify the three usages
Look for the explicit statement of the three ways: difference (ikhtilaf), overlap (tadakhul), and synonymy (taraduf). Understand that all are valid in Arabic as figurative usages.
3
Examine the proof for difference
Ghazali explains difference in detail: Iman is affirmation of the heart, Islam is outward submission. He uses the analogy of touching and the Quranic verse 49:14. See how outward submission alone earns the name 'Muslim'.
This clarifies the common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الحديث عن سعد (۱) انه أعطى رجلا عطاء ولم يعط الآخر ، فقال له سعد : يا رسول الله تركت فلانا لم تعطه وهو مؤمن ؟ فقال : أو مسلم ؟ فأعاد عليه فأعاد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم (۲) واما التداخل فما روى أيضاً أنه سئل فقيل : أي الأعمال أفضل ؟ فقال : «الاسلام» فقال : أي الإسلام أفضل ؟ فقال : ( الايمان (۳) وهذا دليل على الإختلاف وعلى التداخل ، وهو و أوفق الإستعمالات (٤) في اللغة ، لان الإيمان عمل من الأعمال وهو أفضلها ، والإسلام هو تسليم أما بالقلب وأما باللسان وأما بالجوارح وأفضلها الذي بالقلب ، وهو التصديق الذي يسمى إيماناً . والإستعمال لهما على سبيل الإختلاف وعلى سبيل التداخل وعلى سبيل الترادف كله غير خارج عن طريق التجوز في اللغة(۱) أما الإختلاف فهو أن يجعل الإيمان عبارة عن التصديق بالقلب فقط(٢) ، وهو موافق للغة (۳) والإسلام عبارة عن التسليم ظاهراً ، وهو أيضاً موافق للغة فإن التسليم ببعض محال التسليم ينطلق عليه إسم التسليم فليس من شرط حصول الإسم عموم المعنى لكل محمل يمكن أن يوجد المعنى فيه ، فإن من لمس غيره بدنه يسمى لامساً وإن لم يستعرق جميع بدنه فإطلاق إسم الإسلام على التسليم الطاهر [؟] عند عدم تسليم الباطن مطابق لسان [؟] وعلى هذا الوجه جرى قوله تعالى : ( قَالَتِ الأَعْرَابُ آمَنَا قُل لَّمْ تُؤْمِنُواْ وَلَكِنْ قُولُوا أَسْلَمْنَا ) (٤) . ذلك أن الإسلام إنقياد ودخول في السلم وإظهار للشهادة لا بالحقيقة ، ومن ثم قال : قل لم تؤمنوا فإن كل ما يكون من الإقرار من غير مواطأة القلب فهو إسلام .
And in the hadith from Sa'd (1) that he gave a gift to a man and did not give to another, so Sa'd said to him: O Messenger of Allah, you left so-and-so, you did not give him, while he is a believer? He said: 'Or a Muslim?' So he repeated [the question] to him, and the Messenger of Allah repeated [the answer] (2). As for overlap, there is also narrated that he was asked and it was said: Which deeds are best? He said: 'Islam.' He said: Which Islam is best? He said: 'Faith' (3). And this is evidence for difference and for overlap, and it is the most appropriate of usages (4) in the language, because faith is a deed among deeds and it is the best of them, and Islam is submission, either by the heart or by the tongue or by the limbs, and the best of them is that by the heart, and it is the affirmation that is called faith. And the usage of them on the basis of difference, on the basis of overlap, and on the basis of synonymy, all of it is not outside the path of figurative language in the language (1). As for difference, it is that faith is made to express affirmation by the heart only (2), and it agrees with the language (3), and Islam is made to express submission outwardly, and it also agrees with the language, for submission by some of the aspects of submission is called by the name 'submission'; it is not a condition for the occurrence of the name the generality of the meaning in every way in which the meaning might be found, for whoever touches another with his body is called 'toucher' even if he does not cover his entire body. So the application of the name 'Islam' to outward submission when there is no inward submission is in accordance with the language, and on this basis went the saying of the Exalted: 'The bedouins say: We have believed. Say: You have not believed, but rather say: We have submitted' (4). That is because Islam is compliance and entering into peace and showing testimony not in truth, and from that He said: 'Say: you have not believed,' for everything that is of acknowledgment without alignment of the heart is Islam.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic and theological analysis of the relationship between the terms 'Islam' and 'Iman' (faith). The author begins by citing two hadiths: one from Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas where the Prophet distinguishes between 'believer' and 'Muslim', and another where the Prophet ranks 'Iman' as the best part of 'Islam'. These are presented as evidence for two types of usage: difference (ikhtilaf) and overlap (tadakhul). The author then states that there are three possible ways to use these terms—difference, overlap, and synonymy (taraduf)—and that all are permissible within Arabic figurative language (tajawuz). He proceeds to explain the first type, difference, in detail: Iman is defined as affirmation (tasdiq) of the heart only, and Islam as outward submission (taslim) through actions and speech. He argues that calling a person 'Muslim' even when inner faith is lacking is linguistically valid, using an analogy: one who touches someone else's body with part of his own is called a 'toucher' even though he hasn't touched every part. Similarly, outward submission alone earns the name 'Islam'. The author then cites Quran 49:14 as a proof: the Bedouins declared faith, but Allah instructed them to say 'we have submitted' (aslamna) instead, because their submission was outward only. The passage concludes by reaffirming that Islam in this context means compliance and outward show of testimony without true inner conviction.

Key Terms
إيمان
إسلام
اختلاف
تداخل
ترادف
تجوز
تصديق
تسليم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that 'Islam' and 'Iman' are always synonymous or that they are mutually exclusive.
Correction: The passage shows that linguistically, they can be used as different, overlapping, or synonymous — all valid. The difference usage is supported by hadith and Quran.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the three permissible ways the words Islam and Iman can be used in Arabic?
A) Literal, metaphorical, and figurative
B) Difference, overlap, and synonymy
C) Obligatory, recommended, and permitted
D) Past, present, and future

Key Takeaway
The terms 'Islam' and 'Iman' are flexible in Arabic: they can indicate difference, overlap, or synonymy, and all are legitimate linguistic usages within the scope of figurative language.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call yourself a Muslim, is your submission only outward – in your actions and words – while your heart remains unconvinced or negligent? In what specific area of your life does your tongue say 'aslamna' but your heart does not truly submit? What would it take for you to be among those of whom Allah says 'those who believe'?
?
The Prophet distinguished between a Muslim and a believer. If you were to be honest with yourself, which category do you truly fall into right now? Are you content with being merely a Muslim in form, or do you yearn for the depths of iman? What is one habit or attitude you can change today to move from outward Islam to inward faith?
?
The author uses the analogy of a toucher to justify using 'Islam' for partial submission. But in the sight of Allah, is your submission partial? Which acts of worship do you perform without the presence of your heart? How can you transform those empty rituals into true submission that engages your whole being?

6 min
The Overlap (Tadakhul) of Islam and Iman
Al-Ghazali defines tadakhul and illustrates it with a circle analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Iman is a specific part of Islam according to al-Ghazali's concept of tadakhul, illustrated by the concentric circles analogy.

Core Idea
Iman is the inner core of Islam, not the whole.

Al-Ghazali begins by referencing a hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) prefers 'iman' over 'islam' in answer to a question. He then defines the key term 'tadakhul' (overlap), explaining that islam encompasses submission by heart, speech, and action, while iman is specifically the heart's attestation. To clarify, he shares a vivid analogy of two concentric circles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the hadith reference
The passage opens with a hadith about Sa'd. Notice how al-Ghazali uses it to introduce the idea that iman is superior to islam in certain contexts.
The hadith is a prompt for the theological discussion.
2
Understand the definition of tadakhul
Al-Ghazali defines tadakhul as making islam the general term for submission (heart, speech, action) and iman the specific term for heart's attestation. This is the core concept.
The Arabic terms taslim and tasdiq are key.
3
Examine the circle analogy
Read the narration from Abu Ja'far about two concentric circles. The larger circle is islam; the smaller inner circle is iman. Exiting the inner circle means losing perfection of iman but not leaving islam.
This illustrates that iman is within islam, not separate.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider what it means to 'exit from iman' while remaining in islam. The passage clarifies that it is a loss of perfection, not of the name 'believer' entirely.
This guards against the misconception that leaving iman equals leaving islam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله في حديث سعد « او مسلم » لأنه فضل احدهما على الآخر . ويريد بالاختلاف تفاضل المسميين (١) وأما التدخل فموافق أيضاً للعة (۲) في خصوص الإيمان ، وهو أن يجعل الإسلام عبارة عن التسليم بالقلب والقول والعمل جميعاً ، والإيمان عبارة عن بعض ما دخل في الإسلام وهو التصديق بالقلب ، وهو الذي عنيناه بالتداخل ، وهو موافق للغة في خصوص الإيمان (۳) وعموم الإسلام للكل (٤) . وعلى هذا خرج قوله ( الإيمان ) في جواب قول السائل أي الإسلام أفضل ؟ لأنه جعل الإيمان خصوصاً من الإسلام فأدخله فيه (٥) وأما استعماله فيه على سبيل الترادف بان يجعل الإسلام عبارة على التسليم بالقلب والظاهر جميعاً فإن كل ذلك تسليم 6 وكذا الإيمان ، ويكون التصرف في الايمان على الخصوص بتعميمه وإدخال الظاهر في معناه ، وهو جائز ، لان تسليم الظاهر بالقول والعمل ثمرة تصديق الباطن ونتيجته (1) أحدهما على الآخر ، وتفاوتهما في الدرجات والمقامات (۲) فإنه دخول أحدهما في ضمن الآخر (۳) وذلك نظراً إلى التصديق القلبي (٤) وعموم الإسلام للكل : نظراً إلى شموله، من اللسان والقلب والعمل (٥) وروى عن أبي جعفر ، محمد بن علي بن الحسين : أن الإيمان مقصور في الإسلام معناه هو في باطنه ، قال : وأدار دائرة فقال : هذا للإسلام ، ثم أدار في وسطه دائرة أخرى صغيرة فقال : وهذا للإيمان في الإسلام ، فإذا فعل وفعل خرج من الإيمان وصار في الإسلام ، يريد به ، خرج من حقيقة الايمان وكماله ولم يكن من الموصوفين الممدوحين بالخوف والورع من المؤمنين ، لا أنه خرج من الاسم ، والمعنى حتى لا يكون مؤمناً بالله عز وجل مصدقاً برسله وكتبه ، ألا ترى الدائرة الصغيرة غير خارجة عن الدائرة الكبيرة التي أدارها حولها فجعلها فيها لأنها خالصها وقلبها ومخصوصة فيها ولو كان أراد به يخرج من الإيمان لجعلهما دائرتين منفردتين ولم يجعل أحداهما وسط الاخرى ٢٤٢ وقد يطلق اسم الشجر ويراد به الشجر مع ثمره على سبيل (١) التسامح ، فيصير بهذا القدر من التعميم مرادفاً لاسم الإسلام ومطابقاً(۲) له ، فلا يزيد عليه ولا ينقص وعليه خرج قوله : ﴿ فَمَا وَجَدْنَا فِيهَا غَيْرَ بَيَتٍ مِنَ الْمُسْلِمِينَ﴾ هو ؟ (۱) والإتساع فيحتاج في فهمه الى هذا التقدير (۲) جمعا بين المتوافقين وضديهما . (۳) الآية : ( ٣٦ ) من سورة الذاريات ( ٥١ ) . وعليه صح إستثناء المسلمين من المؤمنين
And his statement in the hadith of Sa'd 'or Muslim' because he preferred one over the other. And by 'difference' he intends the superiority of the named ones (1). As for overlap (tadakhul), it is also in agreement with the language in the specific case of iman, and it is to make islam an expression of submission (taslim) by the heart, speech, and action all together, and iman an expression of some of what entered into islam, namely attestation (tasdiq) by the heart, and this is what we meant by overlap. And it agrees with the language in the specificity of iman (3) and the generality of islam for all (4). And upon this is based his statement 'iman' in answer to the questioner's saying 'Which islam is best?' because he made iman a specific part of islam and thus included it in it (5). And as for using it as synonymy (taraduf), by making islam an expression of submission by the heart and the apparent together, for all of that is submission, and likewise iman, and the treatment of iman specifically is by generalizing it and including the apparent in its meaning, and that is permissible, because the submission of the apparent by speech and action is the fruit of the heart's attestation and its result (1) one over the other, and their differing in degrees and stations (2) for it is the entry of one within the other (3) that is, considering the heart's attestation (4) and the generality of islam for all: considering its comprehensiveness of tongue, heart, and action (5). And it is narrated from Abu Ja'far, Muhammad ibn Ali ibn al-Husayn: that iman is confined within islam, its meaning is that it is in its interior. He said: He drew a circle and said: 'This is islam,' then he drew inside it another small circle and said: 'This is iman within islam.' So if one does such-and-such, he exits from iman and enters into islam. By this he means he exits from the reality and perfection of iman and is not among those described and praised for fear and piety from the believers, not that he exits from the name, and the meaning is that he would not be a believer in Allah Almighty and attestator of His messengers and books. Do you not see that the small circle is not outside the large circle that he drew around it, placing it inside because it is its core, its heart, and special within it. If he had meant by it to exit from iman, he would have made them two separate circles and not placed one inside the other. 242 And sometimes the name 'tree' is applied, meaning the tree with its fruit, by way of tolerance (1), so by this degree of generalization it becomes synonymous with the name 'islam' and corresponding to it (2), so it does not exceed it nor fall short. And upon this is based His statement: {Then We found not therein other than a house of muslims} (Quran 51:36) is it? (1) And expansive usage, so understanding it requires this estimation (2) bringing together the two agreements and their opposites. (3) The verse: (36) from Surah al-Dhariyat (51). And upon this it is valid to exclude muslims from believers.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage from al-Ghazali is a detailed analysis of the various semantic relationships between the terms 'islam' and 'iman'. The author begins by commenting on a hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) is asked which islam is best, and he responds by mentioning 'iman'. Al-Ghazali explains that this indicates a relationship of overlap (tadakhul): iman is a specific part of islam, as islam encompasses submission by heart, speech, and action, while iman is specifically the heart's attestation. This usage is consistent with linguistic convention. He then introduces the possibility of synonymy (taraduf), where islam and iman are used interchangeably, both meaning submission with heart and limbs. He justifies this by noting that outward submission is the fruit of inner attestation. To illustrate the overlap, he narrates a story from Abu Ja'far using two concentric circles: the larger circle is islam, and the smaller inner circle is iman. Exiting from the inner circle (iman) does not mean leaving the outer circle (islam); rather, it means losing the perfection of faith but still being within the fold of islam. Finally, al-Ghazali provides an analogy from everyday language: the word 'tree' can be used to mean the tree including its fruit, or just the tree itself. Similarly, 'islam' can be general (including iman) or specific (excluding iman), depending on context. He cites the Quranic verse about the house of muslims (Quran 51:36) as an example where 'muslims' is used in a general sense that might exclude the more specific 'believers'.

Key Terms
الإسلام
الإيمان
التداخل
التسليم
التصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Leaving Iman means leaving Islam entirely.
Correction: The concentric circles show that exiting the inner circle (Iman) still leaves one within the outer circle (Islam), so it is a loss of perfection, not complete exit.

Checkpoint
According to the circle analogy, what happens when a person 'exits from iman'?
A) He exits from Islam completely.
B) He remains in Islam but loses the perfection of Iman.
C) He becomes a non-believer.
D) He moves to a smaller circle.

Key Takeaway
Iman is the inner core of Islam; one can lose the perfection of Iman without leaving Islam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After learning that Iman is the inner circle within Islam, examine your own heart: Are you merely content with the outer circle of Islam—performing actions and speaking words—or do you actively cultivate the inner attestation and sincerity? What specific steps will you take this week to move from the outer circle toward the core?
?
The analogy shows that one can exit the inner circle (losing perfection of Iman) while still remaining in Islam. Have you ever experienced a time when your inner faith weakened but you still performed the outward acts? How did that feel, and what did you do to restore the inner conviction?
?
Abu Ja'far's illustration warns against being satisfied with just being 'in Islam' without striving for the 'iman' within. Is there any area where you have become complacent, assuming outward practice is enough? Confess that now and resolve to seek the heart's true submission.

Faith and Disbelief
beginner
5 min
The Synonymy (Taraduf) of Islam and Iman
Al-Ghazali explains taraduf and illustrates with a tree analogy and Quranic example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islam and Iman can be used synonymously, as shown by the tree analogy and the Quranic example.

Core Idea
When outward actions are included, Islam and Iman can mean the same.

Al-Ghazali continues by explaining that Islam and Iman can also be used interchangeably. He supports this with a linguistic analogy: just as the word 'tree' can refer to the tree with its fruit, 'Islam' can be generalized to include Iman. A Quranic verse provides an example where 'muslims' is used in a sense that might exclude the more specific 'believers'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on taraduf
This part discusses the synonymous use of Islam and Iman. Notice how outward submission is considered the fruit of inner attestation, justifying the interchangeability.
Taraduf is the opposite of tadakhul in some ways.
2
Understand the tree analogy
Al-Ghazali compares the relation to the word 'tree', which can mean the tree alone or the tree with its fruit. Similarly, Islam can be general (including Iman) or specific.
This analogy is key to grasping how context determines meaning.
3
Examine the Quranic example
The verse from Surah al-Dhariyat (51:36) about a 'house of muslims' is cited to show how 'muslims' can be used in a broader sense that allows exclusion of 'believers' in certain contexts.
The passage ends by confirming that it is valid to exclude muslims from believers in such usage.
4
Reflect on the flexibility of terms
Consider how the same words can shift meaning depending on context. This passage teaches that theological terms are not fixed but can be understood through linguistic principles.
This avoids rigid interpretations.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage from al-Ghazali is a detailed analysis of the various semantic relationships between the terms 'islam' and 'iman'. The author begins by commenting on a hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) is asked which islam is best, and he responds by mentioning 'iman'. Al-Ghazali explains that this indicates a relationship of overlap (tadakhul): iman is a specific part of islam, as islam encompasses submission by heart, speech, and action, while iman is specifically the heart's attestation. This usage is consistent with linguistic convention. He then introduces the possibility of synonymy (taraduf), where islam and iman are used interchangeably, both meaning submission with heart and limbs. He justifies this by noting that outward submission is the fruit of inner attestation. To illustrate the overlap, he narrates a story from Abu Ja'far using two concentric circles: the larger circle is islam, and the smaller inner circle is iman. Exiting from the inner circle (iman) does not mean leaving the outer circle (islam); rather, it means losing the perfection of faith but still being within the fold of islam. Finally, al-Ghazali provides an analogy from everyday language: the word 'tree' can be used to mean the tree including its fruit, or just the tree itself. Similarly, 'islam' can be general (including iman) or specific (excluding iman), depending on context. He cites the Quranic verse about the house of muslims (Quran 51:36) as an example where 'muslims' is used in a general sense that might exclude the more specific 'believers'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quranic verse contradicts the idea that Iman is a part of Islam.
Correction: The verse uses 'muslims' in a broad sense that may not imply full Iman, aligning with the taraduf concept.

Checkpoint
What analogy does al-Ghazali use to explain the synonymous use of Islam and Iman?
A) A circle within a circle
B) A tree and its fruit
C) A river and its tributaries
D) A house and its rooms

Key Takeaway
Islam and Iman can be used synonymously when outward actions are included as the fruit of inner faith, as shown by the tree analogy and the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live your Islam as a tree with fruit, or as a tree that is bare? What fruit is missing from your life right now, and when will you honestly begin to cultivate it?
?
When you read the Quranic verse about the 'house of muslims', do you automatically assume they were complete believers? How does that assumption affect the way you judge Muslims around you who may be weak in faith?
?
If your iman were to be separated from your outward Islam today, would there be any fruit left? What would change in your daily actions if you truly acted as if your inner faith is the fruit that must be produced?
